Boy George was once asked how he wrote so many hit songs.  He said he would listen to the top 40 songs and take pieces of the hooks in each song and then add his own verses. I remember learning all the poor African-American songs that inspired so much of Led Zepellin's songs were never acknowledged by the supergroup.  I was studying "When the Levee Breaks" by Led Zeppelin because of the unique state of mind and emotion I felt, the players, the interesting odd mic pickup of the drum echo (a random guitar effects box sitting on the stairs) the lyrics, and the dark infinite shame that the main character begrudgingly told his woman that "I can't take you with me."  I was mentally in the cold mud experiencing the hopelessness that gave me tingles when that lyric hit me.   "This is genius!"  Like the feeling of quality and completeness of Samuel Barber's Adagio for Strings, like watching Stevie Ray Vaughn's band play "Voo-Doo Child" more properly than Jimmy Hendrix. When the Levee Breaks was a perfect recording.   And in my research, I found a recording that pre-dates all while I was watching YouTube.   I'm still broken-hearted.

The scenario has me watching this video. I didn't know about either song prior. Want a potentially interesting rabbit hole to read about? Clustering, explained by Game Theory. Hotelling's Location Model and variation Salop's Circle Model explain mathematically how something like this is great for both parties in the face of competition for listeners or I suppose streams. Think Lowe's across from Home Depot, CVS across from Walgreens, Every car dealer next to another, McDonalds across from Wendy's. Far from being bothered by it, both entities experience INCREASED business when this occurs over being geographically distant. A Nash equilibrium is achieved by both songs competing closely for listeners, but my presence listening to both has now increased the customer base. It would take a grad level paper to explain this, as I haven't found anything to explain the increase in audience from this type of Beato video which profiles the scenario. Some reader of this comment will likely know a lot more than I do. Any way you slice it, this similarity is probably a GOOD thing for all involved.

So what's going to start happening more and more is that people will just create their own thing, and if it turns out that they subconsciously took an idea from  somebody else, they'll just work out a deal to share the credits, because creativity is part of life, so it won't stop.

The "even" part was intentional to match the desired rhythm. a two syllable word had to go somewhere to keep the desired rhythm going. One thing thats changed in todays music is the rhythm of the words comes first, then the words themselves and the meaning of those words comes second. The feel of the sentence is more important than the literal meaning So while some think it makes no sense its actually intentional and not lazy writing

What a lot of people don't understand is that co-writing works differently than just writing it by yourself. And a lot of times, it can cross to music production as well, which is a whole different beast. There are a lot of songwriters and music producers out there, but really just a handful of really good ones. Collaboration is the other way to go. Creativity can be a rabbit hole of fixations and without a collaborator, a good chorus or hook could go to waste without seeing the light of day. Is it cheating? Not necessarily, it it also depends on from which perspective you want to view it from. This one, you should look at it from a pop perspective, nowhere else. And that's what makes it good.

Rick, you should do a segment on rock history plagiarism. It would be revealing to show how bands like the Allman Brothers, Led Zeppelin, Rolling Stones, Boston etc. not only copied the music arrangements of others but also verbatim lyrics of previous artists while giving those artists zero credit. In some cases they did so simply because the copyrights of the original songs had expired. While it is true and fair to say that many artists have been inspired by their predecessors which is ok, there are instances where the copies were quite blatant.

We see this too many times in this "Business", Another classic example is The Rolling Stones vs KD Lang (sic) "Constant Craving has SUCH a GREAT Chorus, there's no reason why we can't "borrow" it-She's just some weirdo from CANADA-NO ONE WILL KNOW!" Now, of course I'm being facetious, here, but still...This is similar to an anecdote a friend related to me when she was dating the original guitarist from Marilyn Manson. She told MM one night that all he was doing was ripping off Alien Sex Fiend. He shot her a look of disbelief and asked " how OLD are you?!!" She was only a few years older than his main demographic. She argued back that he was underestimating not only the musical intelligence of his fans, but also the quality and influence of the music he was SUPPOSEDELY inspired by, assuming it existed in vacuum that no one else would have ever heard!  Bigger international artists (I use that term loosely) have often pilfered works by lesser known artists from other (smaller) countries, or other time periods, ignorantly assuming they have been forgotten. I'm NOT talking about "Cultural Appropriation" here! This is just good ole fashioned David vs Goliath plagiarism. Grrr!!

Since the beginning of music publishing and prior going back to classical music from 100's of years ago composers have been mimicing and embellishing on previous artists works. Elvis ,Led Zeppelin and the Rolling Stones have done plenty of this,lifting word for word lyric lines and cord progressions to "re-invent" a composition. Michael Jackson confessed to Darryl Hall that Billy Jean was "lifted" from the opening groove of "I Can't Go For That". I hope you don't mind he followed. It is all fare game in the world of popular music. The problem arises when money is involved. Look at the predicament John Rafferty ended up in for sounding "too much like himself"

Betcha it happens all the time.    My sister was a nanny for a rather famous singer.  It was a household that had a buzz, lots of musicians etc. hanging around.  One day she was humming a tune and folk asked her 'where's that tune from?'  The sister said she didn't know & didn't know it was a song... so they all got excited and they started to write a song.... about two hours in.... the Dad (sound engineer) walked in, heard them kicking the tune around and said.... 'Isn't that 'Beat It' by Michael Jackson.    And in that moment my sister understood why some songs sound alike, and it mightn't be conscious plagiarism at work....
I mean .. those songs are just so samy, because all these people are oit of ideas and produce the same crap over and over again. These songs are like T-shirts. This is what has bugged me for a long time and will continue to do so I'm sure. I started to notice many years back just how many writers it seemed to take to write one bloody song, especially for artists who don't write there own music, or in this case, artists who barely write anything but like to get their names on the credits. Lol. I know it's all about collaboration these days, and often times many cooks can make a good broth. Everyone get's there bit of the pie etc. But, more often now,  I just find myself thinking, it took four / five /six or in this case nine people? To write that? ... It makes you wonder what the criteria is to actually qualify for having written a part of a song? Like one person says, "We should change that note there", and hey presto, you've got a writing credit. It just boggles my mind, especially with a song like this. I'm sure there are many good pop writers who could individually crap this sort of thing out by themselves in ten minutes. But the other thing that must inevitably happen sometimes with three or four people getting their hooks in to a song, is that any spark of originality one person might come up with is compromised and diluted. I don't know, better in my day, grumble grumble, and other such discontented mutterings. It just seems to me like the more writers there are, the more originality there should be, but it seems to be the opposite outcome.

Wasn't it Elton John who once said  The most creative writer is one who can take someone else's song, change it just enough to make it their own song, and get away with it legally.   Key word "Legally"  In other words, if you don't change it enough you'll be sued by the original songwriter and lose.  So you have to change it enough to get away with it legally.   Key word "legally"   If you don't change it enough just enough to get away with it legally you've just taken your time and energy and written a song where all the money you've made from it is going to go to the original songwriter you stole it from originally. And not you.   So, it takes quite the creative writer to do that.

I'm a boomer who also mostly hates pop/hip hop of today. But sometimes I find it ain't so bad, and at Thanksgiving, it finally occurred to me why.  My niece and her husband listen to nothing but hip hop. The songs they had on during dinner were dreadful. Every low end bass beat made me sick to hear it. I kept thinking that I paid thousands of dollars for noise gates to make sure my PA systems never made any noises like that. Then I realized it was just a low frequency sine wave. No harmonic tones to it at all (not to mention it had absolutely no musical function in the song). It was just like low frequency feedback.  The two songs Beato is talking about here have a better quality low end tone. Not a dead sine wave. That's what makes them listenable, and most definitely danceable.

Nah the bad lyrics isn't the reason pop music is terrible (bad lyrics don't make Black Sabbath terrible), it's the soulless music written by committee and produced by focus groups. It's the fact that it's a patchwork of cliches designed to cynically pander to predictable and predetermined audience tastes for the sole purpose of making money.  Not all "pop" music is made using the junkfood factory method. Some "pop" music is made by the performers because they love it, the same way rock bands do. I'm talking Frankie Lymon and the Teenagers, Eddie Cochran, The Beatles, Bowie, Michael Jackson, Prince, Blondie, Eurythmics, Grace Jones, Bjork etc. That stuff is good because it has some soul to it, and a direct line to the creative instincts and worldview of someone. The performers are the writers and they have to stand behind their work. Even though the genre is "pop" the medium is very different, still band and artist driven, and talent is still part of the creative process.  The same hacks writing music for people like Katy Perry today would have been writing for David Cassidy back in the 60s and 70s and it wouldn't be hard to see how bland and spiritless their songs are. It's a style of "creativity" that doesn't even need a songwriter, just a record label with a popularity algorithm. It works because of the target audience. The reason a room of six 45 year old record industry goons can write hit singles for 12 year old girls speaks more to the power of marketing and the stupidity of 12 year olds than it does to the songwriting talent in the room.  Gotten to the point where my friends and people I play in bands with have started calling pop music "junkmusic". Sadly, even though I can scientifically prove that McDonald's is bad for you, unfortunately the same thing cannot be said for Katy Perry.  I'm 100% certain that listening to Katy Perry makes people stupid, I just can't prove it scientifically.

The vocal phrase you are talking about at the end is a 2/3 New Orleans Clave starting on the & of beat one. 1, e (&)a     2 (e) &, a   (3),e &,(a)    4 e(&)a.  .. syllables in parenthesis denotes Clave phrase....Which starting on the "and" of beat one is accenting every 3rd sixteenth note rounding off with 1 at end.   Dave Dicenso discusses that there are 5 universal claves.  A song usually has one if not more of these that creates a rhythmic phrase that is the "rhythmic key" to each song.  Sometimes in the vocal, sometimes the guitar or bass line, etc.   As a drummer,  understanding this helps to construct beats that create a rhythmic dance in rhythm section... In this instance ,the kick drum four on floor underneath dances with her vocal rhythm on top. Bam...You felt it.  Game changer concept to latch on to.....King Konga alumnus...;).  Love your vids!!
Rick's observations about the undeniability of pop music begs some questions in my mind. I, too, love it now and then. I find the attractiveness of great hooks impossible to ignore even if it feels like a shallow pleasure. But as I listen to Rick analyze these elements, talking about key signatures, tempo, etc., it makes me wonder if there is something encoded in our DNA that makes certain combos "sound right" or if we have been conditioned over the last several decades to subconsciously seek out these arrangements. Hard to test this, of course, because no one grows in a musical vacuum. But producers clearly lean in predictable directions and we succumb to the guilty pleasure without fail over and over.

Rick, I know the primary focus of this video relates to the thin line between heavily influenced song-writing vs. outright duplication, but I found another element in your video that was thought provoking.  I put myself in your shoes and questioned if I would feel the need to apologize for finding great elements or even liking these songs.  Disappointingly, I think the answer is yes.   One nice thing about getting older is we reach a point of not being concerned about "the cool factor".  Even if this isn't my music of choice, there is still something to appreciate.  So why is it that I feel some level of shame?  I thought some more and discovered it's not shame that I'm feeling.  It's actually begrudging acceptance that stems from complete contempt for what has become of the pop industry... out of necessity to remain financially viable.  However, it would be my loss if I chose to completely reject every artist or song competing in this stagnant swamp.  As a writer I don't need any help crafting melody or harmony, and lyrics are personal without any intention of securing approval.  I don't write about love gained or lost.  As a producer and mixer though, there is a lot I can learn from these songs and that's why it would be a mistake to ignore them.  One other final realization, was a sense of d√©j√† vu.  This is the exact same feeling I had as a 15 year old when I heard Graceland by Paul Simon.  At the time, I was part of a silly metal band... the type of band that made parts of the Bill and Ted movies feel like my personal biography.  Acceptance of music that wasn't authentic metal equated to disloyalty and definitely wasn't cool in my circle.  It was a profound growth experience to no longer deny the merits of other mainstream or obscure genres.  Fast forward to the present and I now feel a slight level of embarrassment for finding something commendable in metal, but I'm mature enough to keep my mind open.  Rejecting any form of music because of pre-conceptions would be my loss.  I encourage anyone else who's experienced similar conflicts to root out illogical musical prejudices and reap the growth benefits of an open mind.  Can't help myself... 9 people with song-writer credit...  really???  Talk about rats fighting for the crumbs of a NY sewer hot dog bun.

Dear Rick, You are doing a good job...LOVE.
Pop music is a guilty pleasure.  It should surprise, yet strike a familiar chord in nearly every ear.  Being able to recycle yesterday's progressions yet still sound fresh is every bit an art as it is to write something way out in uncharted waters.  So.... yeah... Katy's team lifted this tune.  But I'm happy they gave a nod to the original team of writers, and the mashup of the two together is actually pretty cool.  I agree that the gingerbread Katy's team layered on it put it over the top, and since they also credit the original writers as a community I feel good about it.    I wish I could credit the guy who first wrote the blues, but nobody can tell me his name.    We all want to be completely unique as songwriters, but a lot of writers don't see the need to also incorporate familiarity.  I'm sure guilty of this.    I remember once mounting a study of pop music, intending to prove it out it as the hollow lowbrow crap that it was.  I wound up becoming a secret admirer, like you.  The first step was to check myself....   I said  "Wait a minute now, why again SHOULD I hate popular music?  Don't I wish I were more popular?   Isn't the hope of writing a popular song partly why I started writing songs?  Maybe I should learn something about what makes songs popular, and try doing some of that instead of trying to be Professor Longhair all the time."

A couple things that make Love You Like That a better song in my opinion:   1. The tempo is slower, which means the beat sits way farther back in the pocket than the Katy Perry song and consequently feels so much more groovy 2. The stylization of Love You Like That clearly has a more defined identity than the Katy Perry song, where the synths are more beefy and prominent in the mix and have much better high/low points than the Katy Perry song, which feels flat and compressed to hell. The drum samples add to this identity where they're clearly attempting a more "huge" sound and an almost Phil Collins-influenced sound 3. The three part harmony that comes in on the second part of the second chorus just kills it in Love You Like That 4. The emotion in Dagny's voice has a much more emotional delivery, as well as being more dynamic in the harmonics and in the changes from the different song parts. 5. Love You Like That doesn't overstay it's welcome, the song is just long enough to communicate everything it wants to do musically, and it does it all at peak capacity; the Katy Perry song has additional chorus bits and prechorus bits that extend it to 45 seconds longer than Love You Like That 6. The drum beat on Love You Like That has more time and effort put into it than the Katy Perry song; where Love You Like That has a beat with bass drum hits on every quarter note as well as some accents on the UH's before the 4's snare hit, as well as a couple of the same beat hits with an aux snare. This contributes to the groove and feel of the song. The drums in Love You Like That are also less dry and more full than the drum samples on the Katy Perry song.  All of these make Love You Like That a more cohesive and attractive song, in my opinion.

Let's be honest. The only real difference is the Katy Perry version includes the supertonic melody line that seems to be everywhere now.
On a bit of a tangent.......I thought it would be interesting if Rick created a history of record production series that would look at different decades of pop music or any particular genre- like rock. For each decade a defining production style could be discussed and how the technology of the day influenced that style (808!), the technological choices available for listening to music, how people listened to music on a social level (ie. individuals on headphones, listening parties) , how they purchased music etc. For example in the 80's my friends and I all had Sony walkmans. Knowing that I am sure producers were conscious that mixes need to sound good on walkmans among other platforms for listening to music. Even car stereos went from mono radios to potentially massive sound systems with a sub woofer in the trunk. Producers over the decades likely accounted for that. Over decades you can here more bass being added to mixes. You barely here a kick drum on early 60's rock. In the 80's some bands- like ZZ Top-on Eliminator- made the kick drum more pronounced so songs sounded more danceable. You could group the production styles by genre instead of decade or a combination of the two. There are a lot of possibilities.

This is actually a really common practice in the music industry these days. We all know that "songwriters" exist but these songwriters can take many forms. They may be career songwriters but many people do it as a side gig. These writers submit their song demos to agencies, who then pass it along to their clients who work in the recording industry. These clients may be managers for big artists, or advertising firms looking for a new jingle.   In other cases, a lesser-known artist may release a song. It is overflowing with potential for success, but the artist does not have the platform to reach the song's biggest audience. A song like this may be brought to a big artist by their manager or producer. In these cases, the original writers are credited on a remake (like what happened with the Katy Perry song above).   It is important to note that many people get songwriting credits, including producers (like Zedd) or musicians because the "writing" credit is also about writing the song structure and making creative choices (not only to do with the lyrics). Another thing of note: This is only half of the story. 50% of music royalties go to the writers and 50% to the publishers. All cuts of royalties for writers come out of their 50%. Also, the writer's share does not have to be divided up equally between everyone credited. This is all worked out on paper (called a "split sheet") and signed by everyone.   If you look at the wikipedia part in this video with this information in mind, there is nothing particularly suspicious about the quote written there. If anything, it just feels like a small piece of a bigger statement.   TL;DR: This video was argued from a consumer standpoint. The reality is that music licensing is a complex business that works far outside of public view. These things are common. While it may seem like the original artist is being ripped off, in many cases deals like these lead to more financial gain for them purely because it's now a Katy Perry song and will be streamed millions.... and millions... of times. Yes, the system is kind of sad. It's a shame that many original artists do not get recognition. There can only be so many celebrities before people stop paying attention.

@Rick Beato As a Producer this kinda stuff fascinates me and I can often spot copies and rip-offs as I'm listening to the musical content and arrangement. Another good example of this:  First listen to (original) US girl band G.R.L. - Ugly Heart, written and produced by same Katy Perry team (Dreamlab & Max Martin etc), then listen to UK girl band Little Mix - Shout Out To My Ex, mainly the chorus which is an exact copy, but also the vocals, the ad-libs, the rhythm, chords, melody, basically the whole production, with just a different verse part.

This alone is actually an incredibly important way to engage the world. These days people have been suited up for cultural warfare; the moment they encounter anything that presents even the most frivolous ethical dilemma they see red. They resort to ‚Äúseek and destroy‚Äù programming and are immune to evidence of any positive contributions of opposing ideas or actions... everything associated with that competing idea is wicked or otherwise contaminated.  Katy Perry is an easy target. Led Zeppelin was guilty of the EXACT same practices(see ‚ÄúDazed and Confused‚Äù and others) but there is nowhere near the amount of eagerness to attack or ‚Äúcancel‚Äù them over it. I regard this as evidence of at least some degree of cognitive dissonance where people‚Äôs taste in music is affecting their logical faculties. In the case of Led Zeppelin, I would argue the same point: the world is a richer place that they took an obscure song and gave it their unique treatment... except in that case I believe they had to be sued to give credit to the original songwriter Jake Holmes.   My broader point is that it takes maturity to see past the veil of personal bias and see things objectively. Bravo. Ripping off crap to make MORE crap.  Pop music in 2020 in a nutshell.  I have said it for years and will continue until some originality comes back, there are INSANELY talented musicians that are performing pathetically poor music probably since the early 2000's at the latest. It really started in the 90's (my era) with the "bubblegum pop" that was such a big thing.  They figured out how to produce, package, sell and repeat so efficiently that all (or at least most) the creativity in music just went away. At least in the most popular and visible music.  Does anyone honestly think there's even a REMOTE chance we get a groundbreaking album like Tommy, A Night at the Opera, or Rubber Soul? 90% of these performers don't even write their own music. Many can barely READ music.  I suppose it's too harsh to say there's NO originality left.  But it's really few and far between.  And I suppose the problem, at least in part, with today's music versus previous eras is that the music from previous eras exists now.  So the pool of available ideas has been depleted. Whereas bands/artists from the past had a much wider pool of creative options to draw from that "hadn't been done before."  So perhaps the comparison isn't fair. But it certainly makes one fear for the future of music.  This got long. Sorry.

Rick - Speaking of "copying", I'd love for you to do a video on Asia's Cutting It Fine versus Simple Minds's Changeling.  The bass-line is nearly identical in certain parts of both songs.  I don't think it's intentional, but it's an odd coincidence.  Changeling was definitely released first.  The full bass-line starts at about 18 seconds into Changeling and the similar bass-line/rhythm guitar parts start at about 1 minute 21 seconds into Cutting It Fine.  Overall, these two songs are completely different.  That's what makes this similarity so interesting.

Interesting. In here, foreign country far far away from the USA (Thailand, to be exact), artists used to copy popular songs from other countries. The fans get to know much later and become upset (and the song becomes meme much later). The production go so far to put their own name in the credits and not even mention the original artist.  One example comes to mind: X-Japan's Say Anything. I've heard that the label dealt with Sony Music Japan much after the song is release.   One thing I've heard is, at that time the artist does not really work on their own song, they only sing over it. The songs are from the production team. The production team usually use a 'reference' to kick-off the new song, but sometimes the producer or song writers just use the reference as-is, and just write new lyric over it. The artist has no knowledge of the song being a copy from another famous song from oversea.  Right now less and less artists do this as the consumers listen to music from around the world, someone will know immediately if the song is a ripped-off. It still happens sometime though.   Also because of what happens in the past, the consumers are now very keen to catch the ripped-offs. They even label some song as a ripped-off even though the song does not sound anything similar. One example is Jason Mraz's I won't give up.

I don't know what's more disturbing; the fact that the "inspiration" was so blatant...or the fact that it took so many people to write it.  As a songwriter, particularly one who agonizes over lyrics, it baffles me to find out how many hit songs...that may or may _not_ be melodically, harmonically, or lyrically complex...took _teams_ of writers to create.  With all due respect to the producers that _elevate_ music to its greatest potential, I can't help thinking that better SONGS wouldn't require so much help.  Once upon a time, musicians couldn't rely on post-production wizardry to make their songs interesting.  You know?  That said, from "Never Really Over" back to "I Kissed A Girl" I love how Katy Perry's songs often involve chromatic walk-down harmonies in the chorus, and...speaking of chorus...I love the edge they put on her voice! The reason you have "nine writers" on songs these days as opposed to back "in the day" is because back then, you brought in a session muso (or five) and they added a "touch" to the song but were never called a writer (and got no credit or money). Also, the producer(s) used to "fix" and shape what were often unfinished or raw songs, again without receiving a writer credit.  So, in a way, the fact that these "writers" are getting credit (and one would assume royalties) for what used to be uncredited royalty free work is a good thing.

There is a great story i heard Prince tell on tv about writing Purple Rain and right away he thought, "i have to get this ok'd with Journey it sounds too much like Faithfully" and he did that, he got an appointment with a few members of the band and played Purple Rain for them before it was released and they were blown away!  For several years now i have been telling a story about how i feel my song Bill was ripped off by Kid Rock on Born Free.. I had picked up the term "born free" from the local Buddhist  head monk of the monastery here in Hamtramck (Detroit), he always preaches that we are all born free.  Also I used born free 1974 often on my myspace and that was put on Kid Rock's t-shirt line.. much more i can say.. I was driver for rock photographer and friend "uncle" of Kid Rock, Larry Garcia for several months of abuse.. long story but at one point i made the mistake of giving Larry my album and there were some other routes as well.. At one point he said he was helping another well known local artist on her album and said he was crafting it after a Linda Ronstadt album.. He did try to hook me up with her and others but he was making 5000.oo a night and i was barely making 50.oo and driving my vehicle.. I finally quit after i asked for the "in between work" that i was promised from the beginning and never got, in between dropping off at events and picking up at night and driving back to Hamtramck where he and Kid own several houses.. That night i finally got in between work but we ended up going to Kid Rock's fight club in Mt. Clemens Michigan and i had to wipe up blood between rounds.. I watched kids giving each other life time damage, i screamed at some to put their hands up because they did'nt know anything they were just human punching bags.. there were families their children watching this shat.. I quit the next day.. It would have been the devil's path to fleeting pop stardom

Everybody in these comments: Pop music...gross! I'm far too cool and musically sophisticated to listen to that.  What utter stupidity. I get the feeling that these people think that pop music producers push a button on a computer and out pops a ready-made hit. Of course, there are some terrible and lazy pop songs around today, but then there are also a fair number of really creative and interesting pop songs that are worthy of respect. If you look at what goes into producing a well-crafted pop song you'd be surprised. I doubt many of the wannabe guitar gods on this comments section could get anywhere near producing something as decent as 'Clarity', for instance. Also, they seem to be conned by the idea of 'authenticity'. Some long-haired beardy type can strum a few cowboy chords and sing in an imperfect manner and immediately that's '"real and gritty and authentic'" and worthy of high praise. Nonsense. Sometimes THAT is just plain lazy! Some pop songs are surprisingly sophisticated even if they are based around common chord sequences. My advice is to get your heads out of your arses and stop defining yourself by the music genres that you listen to. Don't prejudge because you don't like the concept of 'pop'. Listen with honesty and open-mindedness. That's what I love about Rick. If he likes a song, he likes a song. Doesn't matter what genre it is. He's not trying to showboat and prove how cool he is (which ironically somehow makes him cool in my book). There is a valid place in this world for pop music. Yes, even Justin Bieber.

If you are a major artist and/or producer rarely do you touch a song without being 'allowed' to be a co-writer...whether you are or not. It's part of the future compensation you are creating for yourself. If the people who actually wrote the song don't approve of the 'creative equity' split that the artist and producer want for themselves then the song generally won't get released. The public mistakenly believes you actually have to help write a song to be legally credited as a co-writer.

"I guess I could try hypnotherapy 10   I gotta rewire this brain 8  'Cause I can't even go on the internet 11   Without even checking your name" 8  Honestly, within the context of this verse, that lyric is actually good lol There's a  story and meaning to this lyric. A beginning and end, a sentence that conveys something. The way Rick judged it, he took it out of context. It's not Bob Dylan lyric quality, but it's still good. It's clear and has a point. Plus, brain and name actually rhyme. Not a perfect rhyme, but close.   Also, the numbers are the number of syllables for each line. 2nd and 4th line have 8 syllables and 1st and 3rd have 10 and 11 syllables. That's really good writing and difficult to do. That's why they wrote "even" twice, so it matches the syllables. 10 and 11 are not an exact match, but it doesn't matter since a 1 syllable difference can be covered up by the voice. This is very solid writing.  And lastly, they actually gave Dagny writing credits, so it's a copy, but not plagiarism. But at that point, why not just do a cover of Dagny's song? I'd rather do a cover than try to create a copy of an original passing it off as an original. Idk

9 writers is ridiculous indeed BUT it‚Äôs not like Zedd was sitting in a room with Perry in desperate need of 7 other people because they are incapable of finishing (or in this case re-writing someone else‚Äôs song) by themselves. The problem is, none of that matters.  Actually Katy Perry's writing team didn't have to credit Dagny or anybody.  They graciously did so and actually people are getting a LOT of writing credit on this song...who probably didn't write anything at all.  They created an "arrangement."  In Songwriting/Publishing...there is a HUGE misconception that "arrangement" is "the song".  This is why the  Taurus-Stairway To Heaven suit has no legs. Song is defined by "Melody" in an instrumental and "Lyric and Melody" in a vocal piece...PERIOD. ¬†The changes don't matter.  The key doesn't matter.  None of it matters.  You can play that chord progression all you want...and it's not "Stairway To Heaven."  If a 5 year old girl sings "there's a lady who's sure all that glitters is gold" with the original melody, a cappella,  EVERYONE KNOWS it's "Stairway to Heaven."   Where is "Taurus" again?  I can sing ANY melody and lyric over those chord changes...and ... it's NOT "Stairway To Heaven"  OR "Taurus."    While Perry's team may have copied the track...it's irrelevant and NOT copyrightable as SONG.¬† Sure the similarity is very real and intentional in this case.    If Arrangement were Song...then everything is "Louie Louie."  "Wild Thing", "Angel of the Morning" (same writer), "Hang On Sloopy", "Guantanamera", "Game of Love".......this is ONE example...but there are countless in pop music and rock and roll. ¬†  Other examples...Joni Mitchell's studio version of "Woodstock" is the same as "Bad Company" by Bad Company...even down to the Wurlitzer...but then again "Ride Like the Wind" is the same as both...in the chords...and the bass line...it's just changed in the rhythm of the notes.¬†  "Jet Airliner" is "All Right Now" and "Rockin' Me Baby" is "Crossroads".....  I once mused I would do a track called "Please, Mr. Postman, Give Me Gravy on My Monster Mashed Potatoes."  These two songs while they have a similar sounding track and arrangement...they DON'T share similarities in the COPYRIGHTABLE "Lyric" and "Melody".  The Marvin Gaye - Robin Thicke suit was preposterous and I can't believe it went the way it did. The "songs" have zero similarity....so now the idiot courts think a cowbell and party banter defines song?  Same with the guy who gave Tom Petty credit around that time....the "songs" are not even similar.

It's perfectly okay to love pop music even if it's simplistic and shallow (or ripped-off from something else like in this case). I mean, just because you're a gourmet chef and can cook sublime dishes every day, I don't think that automatically means that you should never be able to enjoy simplistic junk food. A lot of people are unable to tell the difference between "I like it/I don't like it" and "It's good/It's bad." You can like stuff that's objectively bad and dislike stuff that's objectively good. And the "objective" part is a whole other discussion when talking about a person's individual enjoyment of a piece of art. Hell, it isn't like simplistic songwriting is unheard of in rock music and it still yielded anthem after anthem.

I remember a few years ago my friend writes music for production for commercials and tv etc. He was slapped with a lawsuit from a guy that played with a very famous Jazz guitarist for years. He had never heard the guy's song. It unfortunately had the same basic melody and was in the same key and started on the same note! He had to hire a music lawyer and she was able to find a couple songs written prior to the plaintiff's song (one by Tom Petty I think?) and then they dropped the lawsuit. Cost my friend $2500 in legal fees and a lot of grey hair.

I think the problem is, that both songs does not  have melody (in the chorus). The whole chorus is on one note. So, if chords, tempo, drum, and melody (what is absent) or notes are the same - so the songs sounds identical. And that the problem with many pop songs, where "sort of  melody" is one note, - they sounds boring. Ok, the one-note-melody, could be made more appearing with strong back melody, virtuoso bass party, or lead-guitar in background. But if one-note-melody  stucks in two standard chords, that `s what I do not call "the song".

I'm writing this at 4:34 of the video so I'll edit/retract if needed (don't jump me, but correct me if I'm wrong), but I think it is a little misleading to just look at the number of songwriters on a song. Let's disregard the fact that this particular song is ripped off.That nine people "wrote the song" just means that nine people received songwriting credits (in varying proportions), not that they equally contributed to the song. If I recall correctly, Bruno Mars received something like 3 percent of the credits to Uptown Funk for adding the singing bass voice/line in the beat. Sure, nine people is a lot, but think of your favourite band where all four, five, maybe six band members are credited on a song. Of course someone had the idea first, and not all contributed in equal measure, but still there is a group of people behind the song.   It is a lot easier to hate on a song when it is written in a board room by a team whose goal it is to reach certain chart positions (or whatever), but if someone adds or changes a single word, they get credit. If a producer calls someone for an opinion on a song, that someone might well get songwriting credits, and suddenly 16 people have ostensibly "written the song". But of course just a few of them were the brains behind the origin of the song, and the rest is more or less legalities. There's ceratinly more to it than just the number of songwriters, and anyway, if the song is good, then who cares?
